# 21:2
## u51f
“Son of a human being” or “Son of humanity or “Mortal person” or “Human.” God calls Ezekiel this to emphasize that Ezekiel is only a human being. God is eternal and powerful, but humans are not. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 2:1](../02/01.md).

## m1q8
This is a command to stare at Jerusalem as a symbol of punishing the people there. Alternate translation: “stare at Jerusalem” or “stare at Jerusalem so that the people there will be harmed” (See: [[translate-symaction]])

## td4a
Jerusalem was far away, so Ezekiel could not see it, but staring in that direction would be a symbol of harming it. See how you translated a similar phrase in [Ezekiel 6:2](../06/02.md) Alternate translation: “Turn toward Jerusalem and stare” or “stare toward Jerusalem so that the people there will be harmed” (See: [[translate-symaction]])

# 21:3
## is8f
This speaks of Yahweh causing these people to die as if he actually killed them with his own sword. Alternate translation: “I am opposed to you, and it will be as though I pull my sword from its sheath to kill both the righteous and the wicked people among you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## k8kd
“righteous people and wicked people.” This refers to multiple people, not just one righteous person and one wicked person.

## ch9n
something that holds and covers a sword when no one is using it

## vyg4
This is a euphemism that means to kill. Alternate translation: “kill” (See: [[figs-euphemism]])

# 21:4
## xv4h
See [[EZK-xv4h]]
## l13c
This is a euphemism that means to kill. Alternate translation: “kill” (See: [[figs-euphemism]])

## x4hz
This refers to righteous and wicked people. Alternate translation: “those who are righteous … those who are wicked” or “the righteous people … the wicked people” (See: [[figs-nominaladj]])

## g8nl
“among you”

## yc2b
This speaks of Yahweh causing these people to die as if he actually killed them with his own sword. Alternate translation: “it will be as though I pull my sword from its sheath and strike” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## bm2n
Here “flesh” is a metonym for “people.” Alternate translation: “all people” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## w4nb
This is a merism that refers to the area to the north, and to the south, and all places in between. Alternate translation: “in every direction” or “everywhere” (See: [[figs-merism]])

# 21:5
## e2el
This speaks of Yahweh causing people to die as if he actually killed them with his own sword. Alternate translation: “it is as though I, Yahweh, have struck people with my sword” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## ju9i
This speaks of Yahweh not holding back his sword from attacking people as if his sword were a person that was no longer holding itself back from attacking. Alternate translation: “and I will not put it back in its sheath again” or “I will not hold back from attacking people” (See: [[figs-personification]])

# 21:6
## ztb6
See [[EZK-ztb6]]
## ius9
Yahweh tells Ezekiel to groan as a sign to accompany his message. He tells him to groan deeply as if his abdomen was in great pain. “groan deeply as if your loins were in great pain” or “groan deeply with great sorrow” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## xg8x
“With great grief” or “With great sorrow”

## n99h
Here the Israelites are referred to by their “eyes” to emphasize what they see. Alternate translation: “before them” or “before the Israelite people” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 21:7
## jri2
This speaks of the “news” as if it were a person that was soon coming to them. Alternate translation: “the news that they will soon hear” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## c8df
This speaks of people becoming afraid, as if their hearts were fainting. Also, this speaks of the people fainting as a result of fear. Alternate translation: “for everyone will become fearful” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## kml3
“will be weak”

## t44k
This speaks of people becoming fearful in their spirits as if their spirits were about to faint. Alternate translation: “Everyone will be fearful in their inner being” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## p1p8
“every knee will be as weak as water.” This is a euphemism for people being so afraid that they lose their bladder control and urinate on themselves. Alternate translation: “every leg will be wet with urine” or “everyone will lose control of their urine” (See: [[figs-euphemism]])

## nfk2
Yahweh speaks of himself by name to express the certainty of what he is declaring. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 5:11](../05/11.md). Alternate translation: “this is what the Lord Yahweh has declared” or “this is what I, the Lord Yahweh, have declared” (See: [[figs-123person]])

# 21:8
## jft4
This is an idiom that is used to introduce something that God told his prophets or his people. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 3:16](../03/16.md). Alternate translation: “Yahweh spoke this message” or “Yahweh spoke these words” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 21:9
## u3s4
This passage describes the sword of Yahweh which is a metaphor for an enemy army that Yahweh will use to attack Jerusalem. This metaphor begins here and continues through [Ezekiel 21:17](../21/17.md). (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## luz5
This phrase indicates that the sword is ready for someone to use it. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “It is sharp and polished” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## mne4
Someone made the sword smooth, shiny, and clean by rubbing it with a rough material.

# 21:10
## l1f2
Yahweh continues to describe the sword of Yahweh which is a metaphor for the enemy army that Yahweh will use to attack Jerusalem. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## f22c
These can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “I will sharpen it … I will polish it so that it will” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## d5jv
This means that the sword will be so shiny that light reflects off of it easily and brightly. Alternate translation: “so that it flashes like lightning” or “so that light reflects off of it as brightly as lightning” (See: [[figs-simile]])

## l8t7
This rhetorical question emphasizes that the people of Israel will not celebrate their king’s power, because it cannot resist the “sword.” Alternate translation: “The people of Judah will not celebrate about their king’s scepter.” or “we should not celebrate the power of our king’s scepter.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## vq6m
The word “we” refers to Ezekiel and the people of Israel. (See: [[figs-exclusive]])

## kyj2
Here Yahweh refers to the king of Judah as his son. The king’s scepter represents his rule as king. It is important to maintain the image of the king’s scepter here, as the words “every such rod” refer to the scepter. Alternate translation: “in the king’s scepter” or “in the power that the king’s scepter represents” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## h3tv
Here the “sword” is personified as coming to attack the people. This speaks of the king not being able to resist the “sword”, as if the “sword” were hating the king’s scepter. Alternate translation: “the sword will overpower his scepter like that of any other ruler” or “for the king will not be able to resist when the sword is brought against him” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## d6gp
This phrase is used to mock the king’s scepter by calling it a “rod.” Alternate translation: “such scepter” (See: [[figs-irony]])

# 21:11
## z7un
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “I will polish the sword” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## p6m7
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “then the right person will hold it in his hand” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## h6tw
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “it is ready for the killer to use” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 21:12
## u2r3
Yahweh continues to describe the sword of Yahweh which is a metaphor for the enemy army that Yahweh will use to attack Jerusalem. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## l5al
This speaks of Yahweh’s sword attacking his people as if the sword itself were the attacker. Alternate translation: “I will use my sword to kill my people! I will bring it against all” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## t9ym
This speaks of the leaders of Israel being killed with a sword as if they are thrown against the sword. Alternate translation: “They are killed with the sword along with my people” or “The sword will kill them along with my people” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## xzv7
In Ezekiel’s culture, slapping one’s thigh was a sign of grief. Some other translation use the gesture of beating one’s breast which is a sign of grief in many cultures. Alternate translation: “beat your chests” (See: [[translate-symaction]])

# 21:13
## w1lx
Here the king of Judah is represented by his scepter. Alternate translation: “the king” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## z8iw
This is an idiom. Alternate translation: “cannot resist” or “is destroyed” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 21:14
## wn2d
Yahweh continues to describe the sword of Yahweh which is a metaphor for the enemy army that Yahweh will use to attack Jerusalem. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## qub8
This means for him to clap his hands as a sign of his grief. Alternate translation: “clap your hands to show that you are very sad” (See: [[translate-symaction]])

## hyy2
This is an idiom that means multiple times. Alternate translation: “again and again” (See: [[translate-ordinal]] and [[figs-idiom]])

## mc1p
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “It is a sword for slaughtering people” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## py3r
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “It is a sword for slaughtering many people” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## spp8
“attacking them from every side.” Some Bibles translate this as “which enters into their rooms.” This means no matter where they hide, the sword will kill them.

## d9nr
The word “them” refers to the people of Jerusalem and of the land of Israel.

# 21:15
## s461
Yahweh continues to describe the sword of Yahweh which is a metaphor for the enemy army that Yahweh will use to attack Jerusalem. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## r7xl
This speaks of causing the people to be so terrified that it is as if their hearts were melting. Alternate translation: “to fill them with terror” or “to cause them to be terrified” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## u5ry
Here “fallen” refers to the people killed in battle. This means there will be a greater number of people killed. Alternate translation: “to kill many people among them” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## h1ty
Here the sword of Yahweh represents men with swords that are to attack Jerusalem. Also, the word “slaughter” can be expressed as a verb. Alternate translation: “placed soldiers armed with swords at their gates, ready to slaughter the people” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## r14m
The word “their” refers to the people of Jerusalem.

## tdr2
This compares how the sword is well polished and how it reflects light to appear like lightning. Alternate translation: “My sword is polished and flashes like lightning” or “It is polished and reflects light as being like lightning” (See: [[figs-simile]])

## r9yh
The sword of Yahweh represents the men with swords that are to attack Jerusalem. Alternate translation: “released to slaughter the people” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 21:16
## vhe8
Here Yahweh speaks to those who will attack his people as if they could hear him and as if they were a sword that they would use in the attack. He does this to emphasize that he is in control of what is happening during the attack. The phrase “wherever your face is turned” is an idiom for “wherever you desire to go.” Alternate translation: “I tell those attacking with swords, ‘Strike to the right! … Attack in every direction.’” (See: [[figs-apostrophe]] and [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-idiom]])

# 21:17
## asm7
“clap my hands.” Here clapping hands is a sign of triumph. Alternate translation: “clap my hands in triumph” (See: [[translate-symaction]])

# 21:18
## nz8n
The phrase “the word of Yahweh came” is an idiom that is used to introduce something that God told his prophets or his people. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 18:1](../18/01.md). Alternate translation: “Yahweh spoke to me again” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 21:19
## m4nn
The word “sword” is a metonym for soldiers who kill people using swords. Alternate translation: “the soldiers of the king of Babylon” or “the Babylonian army” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## ym34
This is a sign posted where the road divides into two roads. Alternate translation: “a sign will be posted where the road divides into two roads” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 21:21
## wf4x
See [[EZK-wf4x]]
## g5fx
Both of these phrases refer to the place where the two roads meet. Alternate translation: “at the crossroads” (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

## n1gp
These are divination practices.

# 21:22
## ccu9
This means the he will hold one of the articles of his divination that was read as an omen against Jerusalem in his right hand. Alternate translation: “In his right hand will be an article of divination with an omen against Jerusalem” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## iw1d
A battering ram was a cut tree or large log with a sharpened end or an end covered in metal. It was held by several men who would pound the end against a wall.

## q1zh
These were large dirt ramps that the Babylonian soldiers built so that they could get onto the walls of Jerusalem.

## nzg7
These were wooden towers that the Babylonian soldiers built around Jerusalem to enable them to shoot arrows over the walls of Jerusalem.

# 21:23
## a1iv
The eyes represent seeing, and seeing represents thoughts or judgment. Alternate translation: “in the thoughts of the people in Jerusalem” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 21:24
## b96q
See [[EZK-b96q]]
## g6jh
“Because you remind me of your iniquity”

## w4vc
This can be stated in active form. The “enemy’s hand” represents the enemy’s control. Alternate translation: “your enemy will take you in his hand” or “your enemies will capture you and take you into captivity” (See: [[figs-activepassive]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

# 21:25
## ihj3
See [[EZK-ihj3]]
## x5c4
This is an idiom. Alternate translation: “whom Yahweh will now punish” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## zxc1
“at the time when Yahweh ends iniquity”

# 21:26
## h94t
“the king’s turban.” A turban is a beautiful piece of cloth that kings wore wrapped on their heads as a symbol of their authority.

## fwf2
This refers to people with low status and people who are exalted. Alternate translation: “those who are lowly … those who are exalted” or “those with low status … those with high status” (See: [[figs-nominaladj]])

# 21:27
## qcp3
Here the crown represents the position of king. The kingship will not be restored until the Messiah comes. Alternate translation: “There will no longer be a king” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## ngj1
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “the one comes who I assign” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 21:28
## s9mt
The word “disgrace” can be expressed as a verb. Alternate translation: “about how he will disgrace them” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## wl2n
This refers to the sword of Yahweh which is a metaphor for the soldiers that Yahweh uses to kill a large number of people who have been disobedient to him. Alternate translation: “Swords! My soldiers have drawn their swords! They are sharpened for the slaughter” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## t6s4
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “is out of its sheath”

## b94s
This speaks of the soldiers killing people as if they were animals devouring their prey. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “It is sharp in order to slaughter and to kill people”

## js4d
This means that the sword is so shiny that light reflects off of it easily and brightly. Alternate translation: “and light reflects off of it as brightly as lightning” (See: [[figs-simile]])

# 21:29
## x4ak
“empty visions for you Ammonites … with lies for you Ammonites.” The word “you” refers to the people of Ammon. In the original Biblical language, the word “your” is feminine singular and refers to the nation of Ammon. Nations were often thought of as a woman. (See: [[figs-gendernotations]])

## qi4r
This speaks of visions being false and meaningless as if they were empty containers. Alternate translation: “visions that are not true” or “false visions” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## gji8
This refers to wicked people. Alternate translation: “those who are wicked” (See: [[figs-nominaladj]])

## la5c
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “who are about to die” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## a8rs
The phrases “day of” and “time of” are idioms. Alternate translation: “for the time has come for them to be punished and for their iniquity to end” or “for the time has come for me to punish them and for them to no longer be able to commit wickedness” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 21:30
## n3xh
See [[EZK-n3xh]]
## r5ab
“Later the sword will be put back in its sheath.” This speaks of the soldiers withdrawing from attacking Jerusalem, by saying that Yahweh’s sword is put back in its sheath. Alternate translation: “But later the soldiers will return their swords to their sheaths, for the time of slaughter will be over” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-explicit]])

## ge15
This is something that holds and covers a sword when no one is using it. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 21:3](../21/03.md).

## g9uy
The word “creation” may be expressed as a verb. Alternate translation: “In the place that I created you” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 21:31
## dm1x
This speaks of Yahweh punishing the Babylonians because of his indignation against them as if his indignation were a liquid that he poured out of a container upon them. Alternate translation: “I will punish you because of indignation against you” or “Out of my anger I will punish you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## iui1
This compares Yahweh’s rage to a consuming fire. Alternate translation: “I will bring my rage upon you like a blazing fire” or “I will punish you in my fierce anger” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## nc5u
The “hand” of the men refers to their control. Alternate translation: “the control of cruel men” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## z1g9
“men who cause great destruction”

# 21:32

## cv2j
“The fire will burn up your bodies”

## w6d5
“Your blood will soak into the ground of your land”

## h97t
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “No one will remember you” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 22:intro
## v9yg
See [[EZK-v9yg]]
# 22:1
## r5l7
This is an idiom that is used to introduce something that God told his prophets or his people. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 3:16](../03/16.md). Alternate translation: “Yahweh spoke this message” or “Yahweh spoke these words” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 22:2
## v6kn
This rhetorical question functions as a command. AT. “Son of man, go and judge. Go and judge the city of blood.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## eh7w
“son of a human being” or “son of humanity.” God calls Ezekiel this to emphasize that Ezekiel is only a human being. God is eternal and powerful, but humans are not. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 2:1](../02/01.md). Alternate translation: “mortal person” or “human”

## a7f3
The word “blood” is a metonym for murder. Alternate translation: “the city where many people murder their neighbors” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## jzh2
The word “her” refers to Jerusalem. Cities were often thought of as a woman. (See: [[figs-gendernotations]] and [[figs-personification]])

# 22:3
## j2cg
The word “her” refers to Jerusalem. Cities were often thought of as a woman. This continues through verse 32. (See: [[figs-gendernotations]] and [[figs-personification]])

## u3b4
Here the “city” represents the people who live there. Alternate translation: “The people who live in this city pour … its midst” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## v7a4
The word “blood” is a metonym for murder. Alternate translation: “where many people murder their neighbors” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## j6ka
This refers to the time when Yahweh will destroy them. Alternate translation: “so that the time of her destruction may come” or “so that the time may come for her to be destroyed” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## ujd2
A person who God considers spiritually unacceptable or defiled is spoken of as if the person were physically unclean. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 22:4
## lth2
See [[EZK-lth2]]
## u2vl
This speaks of murdering people as pouring out blood. Alternate translation: “because you murdered innocent people” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## lxu6
A person whom God considers spiritually unacceptable or defiled is spoken of as if the person were physically unclean. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## b7gh
The images of days being brought to a close and years coming to an end are both idioms representing ending by death or by destruction. By saying that Jerusalem has brough her days to a close, Yahweh implies that Jerusalem would soon be destroyed because of the people’s sin. Alternate translation: “Because of these things you have done, you are approaching the end of your time” or “You are bringing your existence to an end” (See: [[figs-idiom]] and [[figs-explicit]] )

## tl1y
“have brought your days to an end” or “have ended your life”

## u8pd
This can be reworded so that the nouns “reproach” and “laughingstock” are expressed as verbs. Alternate translation: “I will make the nations reproach you and all the lands ridicule you” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## q6vv
someone whom people disapprove of or rebuke

## gu6t
a person whom people ridicule or mock

## t4k2
This generalization refers to the places who are familiar with Jerusalem and represents the people who live in these places. Alternate translation: “of the people of every nearby land” (See: [[figs-hyperbole]])

## i3ws

## jp3g
“Look” or “Listen” or “Pay attention to what I am about to tell you”

Yahweh speaks to the city of Jerusalem itself as if it were a woman who could hear him speak. Alternate translation: “come to Jerusalem” (See: [[figs-apostrophe]])

## gx6j
This speaks of murdering people as pouring out blood. Alternate translation: “to murder people” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 22:7
## q7wc
“The rules of Israel have dishonored their fathers”

## b8l5
Yahweh speaks to the city of Jerusalem itself as if it were a woman who could hear him speak. Alternate translation: “within Jerusalem … in the midst of Jerusalem … within Jerusalem.” The words “you” and “your” refer to Jerusalem.

## rd1h
“oppressed”

# 22:9
## x1yw
Here “the mountains” refer to the altars on the mountains that are dedicated to idols. They men eat meat that has been sacrificed to the idols in order to be have the blessing of false gods. Alternate translation: “they eat the meat that has been sacrificed to idols” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## n1r9
“do evil things among you”

# 22:10
## e53w
See [[EZK-e53w]]
## zj5w
In all instances of this phrase, the writer refers to the people of Jerusalem as if they were the city itself, and of the city of Jerusalem as if it were a woman. Alternate translation: “within this city” or “among you people of Jerusalem” (See: [[figs-apostrophe]] and [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## r52h
This means that a son sleeps with his father’s wife. Alternate translation: “there are men who sleep with their father’s wife” (See: [[figs-euphemism]])

## d8i1
These are all ways to express that the men have had slept with women they should not have and how sinful their actions are. Alternate translation: “They have raped unclean women during their menstrual period” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## r6h7
A person whom God considers spiritually unacceptable or defiled is spoken of as if the person were physically unclean. Women were considered to be unclean during their menstrual period. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 22:11
## i4cb
These are all ways to express that the men have had slept with women they should not have and how sinful their actions are. Alternate translation: “commit abominations by sleeping with their neighbors’ wives … make their own daughters-in-law shamefully unclean by sleeping with them … rape their own sisters” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 22:12
## n8h5
This speaks of murdering people as pouring out blood. Alternate translation: “to murder people” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## tf3b
This word refers to the money paid by a person to use borrowed money. However, some modern versions interpret “interest” in this passage as “too much interest.”

## zdw2
This means that they have oppressed their neighbors by charging them too much interest. Alternate translation: “you have oppressed your neighbors and made them poor” or “you have made your neighbors poor by charging too much interest” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## jp8b
Refusing to obey Yahweh is like forgetting that he exists. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## al1x
Yahweh speaks of himself by name to express the certainty of what he is declaring. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 5:11](../05/11.md). Alternate translation: “this is what the Lord Yahweh has declared” or “this is what I, the Lord Yahweh, have declared” (See: [[figs-123person]])

# 22:13
## g7vk
See [[EZK-g7vk]]
## b8at
“I have shaken my fist against” or “I have clapped my hands against.” This is a symbolic action that shows anger and disapproval. Alternate translation: “I have shown my anger and disapproval against” (See: [[translate-symaction]])

## vvt6
“the murder that people commit in you”

# 22:14
## i494
Yahweh uses this rhetorical question to emphasize how hurt they will be when he punishes them. This question may be written as a statement. Alternate translation: “Your heart will not stand and your hands will not be strong on the days when I myself will deal with you.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## se71
Here the whole person is referred to by his “heart” to emphasize his emotions and will. Here the idea of “standing” is an idiom that means to be courageous. Alternate translation: “Will you stand” or “Will you be courageous” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]] and [[figs-idiom]])

## ajb4
Here the whole person is referred to by his “hands.” Alternate translation: “will you be strong” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 22:15
## q5cz
These two phrases mean basically the same thing. See how you translated a similar phrase in [Ezekiel 12:15](../12/15.md). Alternate translation: “So I will cause them to separate from each other and live in different nations”(See: [[figs-parallelism]])

## h23a
remove something unwanted

## zmj9
A person who God considers spiritually unacceptable or defiled is spoken of as if the person were physically unclean. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## e3bn
Here the “nations” refer to the people who live in those places. Alternate translation: “the people in other nations” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 22:16
## ka57
Here the “nations” refer to the people who live in those places. The “eyes” represent seeing, and seeing represents thoughts or judgment. Alternate translation: “So the nations will consider you unclean” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## y3i5
When Yahweh says that people will know that he is Yahweh, he is implying that they will know that he is the one true God who has supreme authority and power. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 6:7](../06/07.md). Alternate translation: “understand that I am Yahweh, the one true God” or “realize that I, Yahweh, have supreme power and authority” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 22:17
## zqj8
This is an idiom that is used to introduce something that God told his prophets or his people. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 3:16](../03/16.md). Alternate translation: “Yahweh spoke this message” or “Yahweh spoke these words” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 22:18
## nd5i
The word “house” is a metonym for the family that lives in the house, in this case the Israelites, the descendants of Jacob over many years. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 3:1](../03/01.md). Alternate translation: “the Israelites have” or “the Israelite people group has” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## q3kz
Dross is the impurities that are left over after silver or gold has been purified in a furnace. Here Yahweh speaks of the people being worthless to him as if they were dross. Alternate translation: “has become as worthless to me as dross” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## apn9
Yahweh continues to speaks about how the people have become worthless to him as if they were dross. Alternate translation: “All of them are as worthless as the leftover of bronze and tin, and iron and lead that remain after you melt silver in the furnace” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 22:19
## g5k4
“look” or “listen” or “pay attention to what I am about to tell you”

# 22:20
## c4pc
See [[EZK-c4pc]]
## z88f
The phrases with “anger” and “wrath” are used together to emphasize that he was extremely angry. Alternate translation: “Because of my great anger and wrath, I will gather you” or “I will be furiously angry with you, and I will gather you” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 22:21
## z69y
This is the image of a person gathering the metals in the furnace and blowing the fire to make it hotter. Yahweh speaks of the greatness of his wrath as if it were a fire that he was blowing to make it hotter. Alternate translation: “I will gather you, and my wrath will be like a fire that I will blow on you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 22:22
## mk5l
The word “it” refers to Jerusalem.

## mim9
God speaks of punishing the people as if his wrath were a liquid that he would pour on them. Alternate translation: “have punished you in my anger” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

a secret plan made by two or more people to do something harmful or illegal

## x5z7

## irv7
The word “her” refers to Israel. They “make many widows” by killing married men. Alternate translation: “they make many women into widows by killing their husbands” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## j2q1

## g2wa

## pyq5

## nr1r

# 23:25
## i8jn

## uwb6
This describes the punishment in Babylon for married women who slept with men that are not their husbands. Alternate translation: “They will punish you as an adulteress, by cutting of your noses and your ears” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## f1n5
The word “fall” is a euphemism for “die.” The word “sword” is a metonym for soldiers who kill with swords. Alternate translation: “men will kill your survivors with their swords” (See: [[figs-euphemism]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

## ay3m
This speaks of the survivors being burned by fire as if the fire were an animal attacking and eating them. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “your survivors will be burned by fire” or “fire will burn your survivors” (See: [[figs-personification]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## i1mg
Ezekiel speaks of Oholibah, who represents Judah ([Ezekiel 23:4](../23/04.md)), as if she were a prostitute sleeping with the men of many nations for money. He wants the people of Judah to understand that Yahweh to punish them because they were worshiping the idols of other nations so they could get those nations’ wealth and power. Alternate translation: “acting like a prostitute, lusting after men of other nations” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## q7hx

## pw5b
“the cup that causes horror and devastation.” The words “horror” and “devastation” share similar meanings here and emphasize how terrible her punishment will be. Alternate translation: “for what is in that cup causes horror and devastation” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## t7dj
Oholibah’s sister Oholah represents Samaria. Samaria is called by it’s name but still referred to as a sister. The cup is a symbol for the punishment that she received. ## e7xf

# 24:2
## i79z
“Son of a human being” or “Son of humanity.” God calls Ezekiel this to emphasize that Ezekiel is only a human being. God is eternal and powerful, but humans are not. Alternate translation: “Mortal person” or “Human”

## ve79
The army of Babylon is referred to by its leader. Alternate translation: “the army of the king of Babylon has besieged” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 24:3
## i8ft
See [[EZK-i8ft]]
## xw29
This is a metonym for the family that lives in the house, in this case the Israelites, the descendants of Jacob over many years. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 2:5](../02/05.md). Alternate translation: “this rebellious people” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 24:4
## cet6
Here “food” refers specifically to meat. Alternate translation: “Place pieces of meat” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 24:5
## g2dh
Here the “flock” refers to sheep, not birds.

## h692
Some cultures add bones to a fire because they burn longer than wood. This refers to the bones that remained after the best bones were placed in the pot. Alternate translation: “place the rest of the bones under the pot to fuel the fire” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 24:6
## bvi4
See [[EZK-bvi4]]
## j2jd
Here “blood” is a reference to “murder.” Alternate translation: “the city of murderers” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## l7p7
Yahweh continues to compare Jerusalem to a cooking pot. Alternate translation: “it is like a cooking pot” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## u69f
the red material that forms on metal. Rust eats away at metal and eventually destroys it

## t39c
Yahweh does not give this command to a specific person. This is a general command given to an unspecified person within this metaphor.

## wge9
Casting lots was a way of choosing which pieces of meat to take out, but because Yahweh wants to take out all of the pieces of meat, there is no need to cast lots.

# 24:7
## dq7g
See [[EZK-dq7g]]
## b2an
This means that the blood from those who were murdered in Jerusalem is still there. Alternate translation: “For the blood of those who were murdered among her is still there” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## e9bt
The word “her” refers to Jerusalem, which is represented by the cooking pot.

## tij3
This personifies Jerusalem as a person who murdered the people and chose where to put their blood. Alternate translation: “They were murdered on the smooth rocks; not on the dirt where the dust would hide their blood” or “Their blood fell on the smooth rocks; it did not fall on the ground where the dust would cover it” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## a5bz
“has put the blood on bare rocks”

# 24:8
## k2au
Here Yahweh is speaking about himself taking revenge on those who murdered the people in Jerusalem. Alternate translation: “in order that I could see it and then be angry and get revenge” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## h9cs
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “so that no one could cover it” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 24:9
## cny3
See [[EZK-cny3]]
## k8zi
Here “blood” is a reference to “murder.” Alternate translation: “the city of murderers” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## b6ad
It is implied that the pile of wood is under the cooking pot that represents Jerusalem. “I will make the pile of wood on the fire under you even bigger” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 24:10
## m3iu
“light the fire”

## vsz6
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “burn the bones” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 24:11
## v897
See [[EZK-v897]]
## tn3f
to burn the surface of something

## f8sl
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “to melt the uncleanness within it and to consume its corrosion” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## pz35
A person whom God considers spiritually unacceptable or defiled is spoken of as if the person were physically unclean. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## nsy1
rust

## g7q4
burned away

# 24:12
## pvf5
Here the word “she” refers to the cooking pot. This is the cooking pot that is metaphorical for Jerusalem. Alternate translation: “Jerusalem has become tired” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## dph2
difficult labor

## bj7t
The phrase “has not gone out of her” is an idiom. Alternate translation: “but the fire did not burn away her corrosion” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 24:13
## i282
See [[EZK-i282]]
## f1bi
This speaks of Yahweh punishing the people in his anger as if it were his “fury” that were punishing them. Alternate translation: “until I am finished punishing you and am no longer furious with you” or “until I have punished you and I am longer angry with you” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 24:14
## ncp2
See [[EZK-ncp2]]
## il2j
“nor will I rest from punishing you.” The word “it” refers to Yahweh punishing the people.

## rdm6
Yahweh speaks of himself by name to express the certainty of what he is declaring. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 5:11](../05/11.md). Alternate translation: “this is what the Lord Yahweh has declared” or “this is what I, the Lord Yahweh, have declared” (See: [[figs-123person]])

# 24:15
## cmi1
This is an idiom that is used to introduce something that God told his prophets or his people. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 3:16](../03/16.md). Alternate translation: “Yahweh spoke this message” or “Yahweh spoke these words” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 24:16
## py3j
This refers to Ezekiel’s wife. Yahweh refers to Ezekiel by the part of his body he uses to see his wife. Alternate translation: “your wife, whom you love very much, from” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

## wi26
“by a disease”

## sgn7
These phrases mean basically the same thing and emphasize that Ezekiel is not to cry about his wife dying. Alternate translation: “you must not mourn nor weep” (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

# 24:17
## yh8j
This refers to his dead wife. Alternate translation: “your dead wife” (See: [[figs-nominaladj]])

## b8pp
a head covering made of a long cloth wrapped around the head

## wg7t
a simple shoe held onto the foot with straps around the ankles

## k28d
In Israel, men would shave their beards to express sorrow, then cover their faces until their facial hair grew back. Yahweh told Ezekiel not to cover his facial hair in order to show that he had not shaved his face to express his sorrow. Alternate translation: “do not mourn by veiling your facial hair” or “do not mourn by shaving and covering your beard” (See: [[translate-symaction]])

# 24:18
## e5nr
“The next morning”

# 24:19
## j5bh
See [[EZK-j5bh]]
# 24:20
## a86e
This is an idiom that is used to introduce something that God told his prophets or his people. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 3:16](../03/16.md). Alternate translation: “Yahweh spoke this message” or “Yahweh spoke these words” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 24:21
## a8px
The word “house” is a metonym for the family that lives in the house, in this case the Israelites, the descendants of Jacob over many years. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 3:1](../03/01.md). Alternate translation: “the Israelites” or “the Israelite people group” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## vx6c
The word “behold” here alerts us to pay attention to the surprising information that follows.

## tq97
“I will desecrate my sanctuary which is the pride … of your soul. Your sons”

## qcv7
This describes the temple as the building that the people are proud of. This speaks of it as being their “pride” instead of the source of their pride. Alternate translation: “the building that you are proud of” or “the source of your strong pride” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## lh1d
Here Yahweh refers to the people by their “eyes.” Alternate translation: “the building that you delight to look at” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

## fa6c
Here Yahweh refers to the people by their “soul” to emphasize their inner feelings. Alternate translation: “the building that you truly love” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

## rw4n
This refers to their enemies by their swords. Alternate translation: “your sons and daughters … will be killed in war” or “your enemies will kill your sons and your daughters … with their swords” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 24:23
## vr9u
Here “melt away” is a metaphor for wasting away and dying. Alternate translation: “you will become very thin and slowly die” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## mm9s
This implies that God will not forgive the sins of these people. Alternate translation: “and I will not forgive your sins” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## me25
This is the sound a person makes who wants help, but who has too much pain or sorrow to speak.

# 24:24
## aqs3
Here the word “sign” refers to something that communicates a special warning to those who see it. Yahweh speaks of Ezekiel and his actions as being this warning. See how you translated this metaphor in [Ezekiel 12:6](../12/06.md). Alternate translation: “So Ezekiel will be a warning for you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 24:25
## ffu4
Here Yahweh speaks of destroying the temple as if it were someone that he was capturing. Alternate translation: “that I destroy their temple”

## bc3v
The abstract nouns “joy” and “pride” can be translated as noun phrases. Alternate translation: “which is what they are joyful about and what they are proud of” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 24:26
## v6zt
someone who has been forced to leave his country because of war or another disaster

# 24:27
## xr34
This can be stated in active form. Ezekiel being given understanding and the ability to speak is represented by his mouth being opened. Alternate translation: “I will open your mouth” or “you will know what to say” or “I will cause you to know what to say” (See: [[figs-activepassive]] and [[figs-synecdoche]])

## t54k
Both of these phrases mean that Ezekiel will speak. In the second phrase it is stated in negative form to emphasize that he will speak. (See: [[figs-litotes]])

## x42v
Here the word “sign” refers to something that communicates a special warning to those who see it. Yahweh speaks of Ezekiel and his actions as being this warning. See how you translated this metaphor in [Ezekiel 12:6](../12/06.md). Alternate translation: “You will be a warning for them” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## v14c
When Yahweh says that people will know that he is Yahweh, he is implying that they will know that he is the one true God who has supreme authority and power. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 6:7](../06/07.md). Alternate translation: “understand that I am Yahweh, the one true God” or “realize that I, Yahweh, have supreme power and authority” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 25:intro
## w37s
See [[EZK-w37s]]
# 25:1
## fc5p
This is an idiom that is used to introduce something that God told his prophets or his people. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 3:16](../03/16.md). Alternate translation: “Yahweh spoke this message” or “Yahweh spoke these words” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 25:2
## hr8s
“Son of a human being” or “Son of humanity.” God calls Ezekiel this to emphasize that Ezekiel is only a human being. God is eternal and powerful, but humans are not. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 2:1](../02/01.md). Alternate translation: “Mortal person” or “Human”

## t1h5
This is a command to stare at the people of Ammon as a symbol of punishing them. Alternate translation: “stare at the people of Ammon” or “stare at the people of Ammon so that they will be harmed” (See: [[translate-symaction]])

## z8cr
Ammon was far away, so Ezekiel could not see the people there, but staring in that direction would be a symbol of harming the people. See how you translated a similar phrase in [Ezekiel 6:2](../06/02.md). Alternate translation: “Turn toward the people of Ammon and stare” or “stare toward Ammon so that the people there will be harmed” (See: [[translate-symaction]])

## ji1x
“the descendants of Ammon” or “those who live in the land of Ammon”

## y2y9
“prophesy about the bad things that will happen to them.” This means to prophesy about the horrible things that will happen to the people of Ammon. See how you translated a similar phrase in [Ezekiel 4:7](../04/07.md).

# 25:3
## h56r
See [[EZK-h56r]]
## s9pp
“Listen to this message from the Lord Yahweh”

## e8ne
“you cheered.” The word “Aha” is a sound people make when they are happy about something. In this case the people were happy because bad things happened to Israel and Judah.

## xrf5
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “against my sanctuary when the enemy army profaned it” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## t975
“over the land … over the house”

## z3wr
The word “house” is a metonym for the family that lives in the house, in this case the descendants of Judah over many years. See how you translated these words in [Ezekiel 3:1](../03/01.md). Alternate translation: “the Judah people group” or “the people of Judah” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 25:4
## q4k4
“look” or “listen” or “pay attention to what I am about to tell you”

## cex8
This speaks of the enemy army conquering the land of Ammon and taking possession of the land and everything in it as if the enemy were taking the people of Ammon as their possession. It is not implied here that the enemy took the people of Ammon as slaves. Alternate translation: “I will cause an army from a land that is east of you to come and conquer you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## erp6
“They will set up tents and live in your country”

## c4qf
It may be helpful to specify the source of the fruit and milk. Alternate translation: “They will eat the fruit from your trees and drink the milk from your cattle” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 25:5
## p11j
Here the phrase “the people of Ammon” refers to the land that belonged to the people of Ammon. Also, the understood information at the beginning of this phrase may be supplied. Alternate translation: “I will make the rest of the land of Ammon a field for flocks” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-ellipsis]])

## jjh8
When Yahweh says that people will know that he is Yahweh, he is implying that they will know that he is the one true God who has supreme authority and power. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 6:7](../06/07.md). Alternate translation: “understand that I am Yahweh, the one true God” or “realize that I, Yahweh, have supreme power and authority” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 25:6
## yv28
These actions are ways to show strong emotions. In this case the people are cheering and showing their contempt against Israel. (See: [[translate-symaction]])

## yi9z
Here the phrase “the land of Israel” represents the people who live there. Alternate translation: “all of the hatred you feel towards the people who live in Israel” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 25:7
## bbl3
“look” or “listen” or “pay attention to what i am about to tell you”

## kzd2
“I will hit you with my powerful hand.” Here Yahweh speaks of punishing the people as if he were literally hitting them with his hand. Alternate translation: “I will punish you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## u2p4
The word “you” refers to the people of Ammon but is also a metonym for their land and their possessions. Alternate translation: “I will allow enemies to defeat you and take you, your land, and your possessions as plunder” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## six2
These phrases mean basically the same thing and emphasize that Yahweh will completely destroy the people of Ammon so that they are no longer a nation. Alternate translation: “I will completely destroy you so that you are no longer a nation” (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

# 25:8
## m7lp
These places refer to the people who live in them. Alternate translation: “the people of Moab and Seir” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## jaw7
“Look at the house of Judah. It is like”

## yax1
The word “house” is a metonym for the family that lives in the house, in this case the descendants of Judah over many years. See how you translated these words in [Ezekiel 3:1](../03/01.md). Alternate translation: “The Judah people group are” or “The people of Judah are” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 25:9
## fnu5
“look! I will open” or “listen! I will open” or “pay attention to what I am about to tell you! I will open”

## fyg9
Here the city of Moab is spoken of with the masculine pronoun “his.” Alternate translation: “starting at its cities” (See: [[figs-personification]])

# 25:10
## h7xa
“I will send the same army from the people in the east who attacked Ammon”

## nvt6
Here Yahweh speaks of allowing the armies from the east to conquer Ammon as if Ammon were a possession that he was giving them. Alternate translation: “I will allow the armies to conquer them” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## qs9u
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “so that the nations will not remember the people of Ammon” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 25:12
## ph9y
Here “Edom” refers to the people who live there. Alternate translation: “The people of Edom have taken” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## hs3x
The word “house” is a metonym for the family that lives in the house, in this case the descendants of Judah over many years. See how you translated these words in [Ezekiel 3:1](../03/01.md). Alternate translation: “the Judah people group” or “the people of Judah” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 25:13
## shy7
Here Yahweh speaks of punishing the people as if he were literally hitting them with his hand. See how you translated a similar phrase in [Ezekiel 25:7](../25/07.md). Alternate translation: “I will punish Edom” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## u34t
“I will make all of Edom … from Teman to Dedan.” These are two cities at opposite ends of Edom. This means that Yahweh will destroy all of Edom. (See: [[translate-names]])

## lfw7
The word “fall” represents being killed and the word “sword” refers to their enemies who will kill them in battle. Alternate translation: “Their enemies will kill them with their swords” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 25:14
## fx1e
Here the “hand” of Israel is a metonym for Israel’s army. Yahweh speaks of taking revenge on the people of Edom as if his vengeance were like a sheet that he covered them with. The abstract noun “vengeance” can be translated with the verb “to punish.” Alternate translation: “I will use my people Israel to punish the people of Edom” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## h1jc
“they will show Edom my anger and fury” or “they will punish Edom according to my anger and fury against the people of Edom”

## jl32
The word “fury” means basically the same thing as and intensifies the word “anger.” Alternate translation: “my furious anger” or “my extreme anger” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## t72s
Yahweh speaks of himself by name to express the certainty of what he is declaring. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 5:11](../05/11.md). Alternate translation: “this is what the Lord Yahweh has declared” or “this is what I, the Lord Yahweh, have declared” (See: [[figs-123person]])

# 25:15
## ikk3
The phrase “from within themselves” is an idiom. Alternate translation: “The Philistines hate Judah and have tried to take revenge against it by their own strength again and again” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## k6d4
Here “Judah” refers to the people who live there. Alternate translation: “to destroy the people of Judah” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 25:16
## f57y
This tells the reader to pay special attention to what follows. Alternate translation: “Look” or “Listen” or “Pay attention to what I am about to tell you”

## y9w5
Here Yahweh’s power is represented by his “hand.” Alternate translation: “I will turn my powerful hand against the Philistines” or “I will turn my great power against the Philistines” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## td5r
This is an idiom. Alternate translation: “I will destroy” or “I will get rid of” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## sc1u
people who lived in the city of Kereth in Philistia (See: [[translate-names]])

# 26:intro
## ya6v
See [[EZK-ya6v]]
# 26:1
## t2ex
“in year 11.” This means the eleventh year after King Jehoiachin was taken into exile in Babylon. Alternate translation: “in the eleventh year of the exile of King Jehoiachin” (See: [[translate-ordinal]] and [[figs-explicit]])

## z8up
“on day 1 of the month.” It is uncertain which month of the Hebrew calendar Ezekiel meant. (See: [[translate-ordinal]])

## qf5l
This is an idiom that is used to introduce something that God told his prophets or his people. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 3:16](../03/16.md). Alternate translation: “Yahweh spoke this message” or “Yahweh spoke these words” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 26:2
## niu7
“Son of a human being” or “Son of humanity.” God calls Ezekiel this to emphasize that Ezekiel is only a human being. God is eternal and powerful, but humans are not. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 2:1](../02/01.md). Alternate translation: “Mortal person” or “Human”

## h3yr
Here the city names “Tyre” and “Jerusalem” represent the people of those cities. Alternate translation: “the people of Tyre have said against the people of Jerusalem” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## e6ch
The word “Aha” is a sound people make when they discover something. Alternate translation: “Yes!” or “This is great!” (See: [[figs-exclamations]])

## ye9y
The people of Tyre use these words to refer to Jerusalem as if it were a city gate through which traders from the surrounding nations pass. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “Armies have broken down the gates of the people” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## a2hy
Here the word “she” refers to the word “gates” which have “turned” on their hinges to open to Tyre. This phrase means that Tyre has now replaced Jerusalem as the city through which the traders from many nations pass. Alternate translation: “The gateway has opened to me” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## xwf1
Here to be “filled up” represents being prosperous. Alternate translation: “I will become prosperous” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## dz8h
“Jerusalem is ruined”

# 26:3
## um27
“Look!” or “Listen!” or “Pay attention to what I am about to tell you!”

## bgn4
Here the word “nations” refers to their armies. Alternate translation: “I will gather armies from many nations that will beat against you like the waves in the raging sea” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-simile]])

# 26:4
## z8j2
Yahweh speaks of how he will cause Tyre to be completely destroyed. Alternate translation: “I will cause the armies to completely destroy the city, and they will leave nothing there” (See: [[figs-simile]])

# 26:5
## eq7r
See [[EZK-eq7r]]
## cu3g
The city of Tyre is spoken of here as if it were a woman. Alternate translation: “Tyre will become” or “It will become” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## m2sf
Part of Tyre was an island. This expression is a metonym for the results of Tyre’s destruction. Alternate translation: “an empty island used for drying fishing nets” or “a deserted island where people dry out their fishing nets” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## x6ai
“Plunder” means things that people steal or take by force. Here Tyre becoming plunder represents its wealth being carried off by other nations. Alternate translation: “the armies of the nations will take away every valuable thing from Tyre” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 26:6
## kv1j
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “The enemy armies will slaughter with swords her daughters who are in the fields” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## mr2n
Possible meanings are (1) “Her daughters” were the young women of Tyre who were working in the fields or (2) “Her daughters” is a metonym for nearby towns and villages on the mainland that supported the main city of Tyre. Alternate translation: “Her daughter communities who are on the mainland” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## rmu1
When Yahweh says that people will know that he is Yahweh, he is implying that they will know that he is the one true God who has supreme authority and power. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 6:7](../06/07.md). Alternate translation: “understand that I am Yahweh, the one true God” or “realize that I, Yahweh, have supreme power and authority” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 26:7
## n4gc
See [[EZK-n4gc]]
## mq6a
“Look” or “Listen” or “Pay attention to what I am about to tell you”

## ri6u
The phrase “king of kings” was a title, meaning that he was the greatest of kings, the king that other kings obeyed. Alternate translation: “Nebuchadnezzar king of Babylon, the greatest king” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## es3k
This emphasizes the great size of Nebuchadnezzar’s army.

# 26:8
## pm5g
Here the word “He” refers to Nebuchadnezzar and is a metonym for his army. Alternate translation: “His army will kill” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## q7kf
Possible meanings are (1) “your daughters” refers to the young women of Tyre who were working in the fields or (2) “your daughters” is a metonym for nearby towns and villages on the mainland that supported the main city of Tyre. See how you translated a similar phrase in [Ezekiel 26:6](../26/06.md). Alternate translation: “your daughter communities who are on the mainland” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 26:9
## dl2k
See [[EZK-dl2k]]
## h14v
See [[EZK-h14v]]
## vu9t
Although the battering rams and tools are spoken of as acting against the walls and towers of Tyre, they would have been used by the soldiers in Nebuchadnezzar’s army against the walls and towers. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## n3a4
“Battering rams” are large trees or poles that many men in an army would pick up and hit against a wall or door so they could break it down and get inside. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 4:2](../04/02.md).

## e4l4
Weapons or instruments made of iron used to tear down the towers

# 26:10
## d7sh
Here Yahweh uses a simile to describe how Nebuchadnezzar’s army will enter into the city of Tyre. (See: [[figs-simile]])

## az2d
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “after breaking down its walls” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 26:12
## j1ma
See [[EZK-j1ma]]
## ydv6
See [[EZK-ydv6]]
## k2sc
The phrases “plunder your riches” and “loot your merchandise” mean about the same thing and are repeated for emphasis. (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

## ep9y
expensive and comfortable

## ky6z
These refer to what is left after the walls and homes are torn down.

## x5ey
“into the sea”

# 26:13
## y7hj
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “No one will hear the sound of your harps anymore” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

When Yahweh says that people will know that he is Yahweh, he is implying that they will know that he is the one true God who has supreme authority and power. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 6:7](../06/07.md). Alternate translation: “understand that I am Yahweh, the one true God” or “realize that I, Yahweh, have supreme power and authority” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## pmu4
The abstract noun “justice” can be translated using the verb “judge.” Alternate translation: “when I judge you” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## n4vt
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “I will use you to show you that I am holy” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 29:intro
## qm8t
See [[EZK-qm8t]]
# 29:1
## nk1i
It can be stated clearly that this was the tenth year of Jehoiachin’s exile. Alternate translation: “In the tenth year of the exile of King Jehoiachin” (See: [[translate-ordinal]] and [[figs-explicit]])

## h48u
“on the twelfth day of the tenth month.” This is the tenth month of the Hebrew calendar. The twelfth day is near the beginning of January on Western calendars. (See: [[translate-hebrewmonths]] and [[translate-ordinal]])

## kjl4
This is an idiom that is used to introduce something that God told his prophets or his people. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 3:16](../03/16.md). Alternate translation: “Yahweh spoke this message” or “Yahweh spoke these words” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 29:2
## xr1u
“Son of a human being” or “Son of humanity.” God calls Ezekiel this to emphasize that Ezekiel is only a human being. God is eternal and powerful, but humans are not. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 2:1](../02/01.md). Alternate translation: “Mortal person” or “Human”

## ere5
This is a command to stare at Pharaoh as a symbol of punishing him and his people. Pharaoh was far away, so Ezekiel could not see him, but staring in his direction would be a symbol of harming him and his people. See how you translated a similar phrase in [Ezekiel 6:2](../06/02.md). Alternate translation: “turn toward Pharaoh and stare” or “stare toward Pharaoh so that he and his people will be harmed” (See: [[translate-symaction]])

## tbn7
“tell the terrible things that will happen to him and everyone in Egypt”

# 29:3
## au6r
“the huge creature that lives in the water.” Yahweh calls Pharaoh a monster that lives in the water. The monster is probably a crocodile. (See: [[writing-symlanguage]])

# 29:4
## g7at
See [[EZK-g7at]]
## g15e
sharp and bent pieces of metal or wood that people use to catch fish and other animals in the water

## f9rd
hard pieces of skin that are on fish, crocodiles, and other animals

# 29:5
## ks6e
This can be stated in active form. The implication is that they would die in the wilderness. Alternate translation: “no one will gather you or pick you up” or “no one will gather your bodies and bury you” (See: [[figs-activepassive]] and [[figs-explicit]])

# 29:6
## uv91
See [[EZK-uv91]]
## csp3
When Yahweh says that people will know that he is Yahweh, he is implying that they will know that he is the one true God who has supreme authority and power. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 6:7](../06/07.md). Alternate translation: “understand that I am Yahweh, the one true God” or “realize that I, Yahweh, have supreme power and authority” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## kq2j
The word “they” refers to the inhabitants of Egypt. A reed stalk is unreliable because it is easily broken. Yahweh speaks of them as a reed stalk because the people of Israel relied on the Egyptians to help them in war, but the Egyptians did not help them. Alternate translation: “they have been as unreliable as a reed stalk” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## n6x3
a plant that grows near water and looks like very large grass

## z2bz
the part of a reed that is long and like a thick stick. People can use them like sticks, but the stalks of reeds are not as strong as wood and can break suddenly.

# 29:7
## nez2
God speaks of Israel relying on Egypt as if they were taking hold of a reed stalk in order to use it as a crutch. Alternate translation: “When they relied on you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## lg5c
The word “they” refers to the Israelites and the word “you” refers to Pharaoh or Pharaoh and Egypt.

## xp6x
Because Egypt did not help the Israelites, the Israelites could not defend themselves against their enemy, the Babylonians. God speaks of the Israelites being unable to defend themselves as if their legs were unsteady. Alternate translation: “you caused them to be like people whose legs are unsteady” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 29:8
## h87w
See [[EZK-h87w]]
## k7e5
“Look!” or “Listen!” This word adds emphasis to what is said next. This is the Lord’s message to Pharaoh. Alternate translation: “Pay attention to what I am about to tell you!”

## s3py
Here “sword” is a metonym for battle, and “bring a sword against you” is a metaphor for causing an enemy army to come fight against Egypt. Alternate translation: “I will cause your enemies to come fight against you” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## pv3c
The word “you” refers to the nation of Egypt. (See: [[figs-you]])

## uq3i
Here “cut off man and beast” is a metaphor for destroying the people and animals. God would do this by causing the enemy army to do it. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 29:10
## tiq2
The word “you” refers to Pharaoh. God speaks to Pharaoh as if Pharaoh were a monster in the river.

## nz8i
“I will put Egypt into the power of desolation and waste.” Desolation and waste are spoken of as if they were people who could be given power over Egypt. Alternate translation: “I will cause Egypt to become a desolate waste” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## r6rs
Here “you” refers to Pharaoh and represents Egypt, the country he rules over. Alternate translation: “your country will become a wasteland” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## b3tw
This refers to all of Egypt. Where these places are can be stated clearly. Alternate translation: “throughout all of Egypt, from Migdol in the north to Syene in the south and the borders of Cush further south” (See: [[figs-merism]] and [[figs-explicit]])

## va76
This is the name of a place in the far northern part of Egypt. (See: [[translate-names]])

## lb1i
This the name of a town in southern Egypt. It is now called Aswan. (See: [[translate-names]])

## vn8c
These borders of Cush were south of Syene. Some versions say Sudan or Ethiopia, the modern names of countries south of Egypt. (See: [[translate-names]])

# 29:11
## g6ym
See [[EZK-g6ym]]
## dk5j
Here “man’s foot” represents people. Alternate translation: “No man will walk through the land of Egypt” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

## x4yx
Here “animal’s foot” represents animals. Alternate translation: “not even a wild animal will walk through it” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 29:12
## b86m
The abstract noun “desolation” can be expressed with the adjective “desolate.” Alternate translation: “For I will make the land of Egypt desolate” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## ef3g
Here “Egypt” represents the people of Egypt, and “scatter” represents causing them to move to other places. Alternate translation: “I will scatter the Egyptians among the nations” or “I will make the people of Egypt go and live among the nations” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

# 29:13
## yv9v
See [[EZK-yv9v]]
## kwt6
Here “Egypt” is a metonym for the people of Egypt, and “gather” is a metaphor for causing them to return to Egypt. Alternate translation: “I will cause the people of Egypt to return to Egypt” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## k5h2
This can be translated in active form. Alternate translation: “among whom I scattered them” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 29:14
## ae9f
The phrase “fortunes of Egypt” is a metaphor for the people of Egypt who had been taken away to other countries. Alternate translation: “I will bring back the people of Egypt” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## c3md
the southern part of Egypt between the Delta and the land of Cush. This is also called Upper Egypt. (See: [[translate-names]])

## ug87
Here “lowly” represents unimportant. Alternate translation: “an unimportant kingdom” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 29:15
## jgg2
See [[EZK-jgg2]]
## bd7q
Here “lowliest” represents least important. Alternate translation: “the least important of the kingdoms” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## ff4u
Here “be lifted up” represents becoming important. Alternate translation: “it will not become important again among the nations” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## grg4
“I will make them small.” Here “diminish them” represents making Egypt weak or unimportant. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 29:16
## p8ei
The abstract noun “confidence” can be translated with the verbs “trust” or “rely on.” Alternate translation: “The house of Israel will no longer trust in the Egyptians” or “The house of Israel will no longer rely on the Egyptians” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## r6x6
Here “house” represents the people of Israel. Alternate translation: “the people of Israel” or “the nation of Israel” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## e48h
How Egypt would be a reminder can be stated clearly. The abstract nouns “reminder” and “iniquity” can be expressed with the verbs “remember” and “sin.” Alternate translation: “when Israel sees what I do to Egypt, Israel will remember how Israel sinned” (See: [[figs-explicit]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## mh8u
Here “turn … for help” is a metonym for “ask … for help.” Alternate translation: “whenever they asked Egypt to help them” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 29:17
## ch2l
This phrase is used here to mark an important event in the story. If your language has a way for doing this, you could consider using it here.

## gqc9
This was after King Jehoiachin and the people of Judah were taken as exiles to Babylon. Alternate translation: “in the twenty-seventh year of the exile of King Jehoiachin” (See: [[translate-ordinal]] and [[figs-explicit]])

## wl2a
“on the first day of the first month.” This is the first month of the Hebrew calendar. The first day is near the beginning of April. (See: [[translate-hebrewmonths]] and [[translate-ordinal]])

## awb7
This is an idiom that is used to introduce something that God told his prophets or his people. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 3:16](../03/16.md). Alternate translation: “Yahweh spoke this message” or “Yahweh spoke these words” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## t2q3
“Son of a human being” or “Son of humanity.” God calls Ezekiel this to emphasize that Ezekiel is only a human being. God is eternal and powerful, but humans are not. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 2:1](../02/01.md). Alternate translation: “Mortal person” or “Human”

## l44j
The kind of hard work they did can be stated clearly. Alternate translation: “to work hard at attacking Tyre” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## xi8j
This refers to the heads and shoulders of the Babylonian soldiers.

## u4f4
Either their helmets or the heavy things they carried on their heads rubbed the hair off their heads.

## c9ej
Either their armor or the heavy things they carried on their shoulders rubbed the skin off their shoulders. Alternate translation: “every shoulder was rubbed raw” or “every shoulder was rubbed bare”

## f8rl
Here “payment” represents the valuable things that Nebuchadnezzar’s army would steal from Tyre as a reward for defeating it. God speaks of these things as if they were what God would pay them for working for him. Alternate translation: “he and his army did not get any valuable things from Tyre as reward” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 29:19
## e2h7
“Look!” or “Listen!” This word adds emphasis to what is said next. Alternate translation: “Pay attention to what I am about to tell you!”

## kl6x
Yahweh speaks of causing the army of Babylon to defeat Egypt as if God were giving Egypt to Nebuchadnezzar. Alternate translation: “I will cause the army of Nebuchadnezzar, the king of Babylon, to defeat Egypt” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## vlc7
God speaks of these things that Nebuchadnezzar’s army would take as if they were what God would pay them for working for him. Alternate translation: “his army will receive those things as if they were payment” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 29:20
## g36f
Yahweh speaks of himself by name to express the certainty of what he is declaring. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 5:11](../05/11.md). Alternate translation: “this is what the Lord Yahweh has declared” or “this is what I, the Lord Yahweh, have declared” (See: [[figs-123person]])

# 29:21
## i2nc
An animal horn represents the animal’s strength, so horn is a metonym for strength. God speaks of making Israel strong as if Israel were an animal, and he were to cause its horn to grow. Alternate translation: “I will make the people of Israel strong” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## uf1d
The word “house” is a metonym for the family that lives in the house. Here it refers to the people of Israel, the descendants of Jacob whom God called Israel. Alternate translation: “the people of Israel” or “the nation of Israel” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

“speak to them”

## aai4
This probably refers to the kingdom of Lydia which was in what is now Turkey.

## p9mu
The word “all” here is a generalization. It means that many people will die. (See: [[figs-hyperbole]])

## f8np

This represents Egypt no longer being proud of their strength. The reason that they will not be proud is that they will no longer be strong. # 30:18
## h434
This was an important city in northern Egypt.

## mtp2

## pwv1
The word “yoke” here refers to Egypt’s power to oppress people. Alternate translation: “when I stop Egypt from oppressing people” or “when I end Egypt’s power to cruelly control other nations” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## e5mh
This represents Egypt no longer being proud of their strength. The reason that they will not be proud is that they will no longer be strong. Alternate translation: “Egypt will no longer be proud about being strong” or “Egypt will no longer have the strength that they were so proud of” (See:[[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-explicit]])

## ylq1
Possible meanings are (1) the dark cloud represents how sad the people of Egypt will feel. Alternate translation: “It will be as if there is a cloud over Egypt” or (2) there will be a cloud of dust over Egypt from everything that is destroyed. Alternate translation: “A cloud of dust will be over Egypt” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## mw36
Possible meanings are (1) the people of the Egyptian towns or (2) the daughters of the Egyptian people

# 30:19
## hci1
The phrase “acts of judgment” refers to punishment. Alternate translation: “I will punish Egypt in many ways” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## q3ti
“so the Egyptians”

# 30:20
## v536
See [[EZK-v536]]
## x8fn
This phrase is used here to mark the beginning of a new part of the story. If your language has a way for doing this, you could consider using it here. (See: [[writing-newevent]])

## tl8c
This is the eleventh year after King Jehoiachin was taken into exile in Babylon. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 26:1](../26/01.md).

## gy39
“in the seventh day of the first month.” This is the first month of the Hebrew calendar. The seventh day is near the beginning of April on Western calendars. (See: [[translate-hebrewmonths]] and [[translate-ordinal]])

## n3p5
This is an idiom that is used to introduce something that God told his prophets or his people. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 3:16](../03/16.md). Alternate translation: “Yahweh spoke this message” or “Yahweh spoke these words” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 30:21
## qjk8
“Son of a human being” or “Son of humanity.” God calls Ezekiel this to emphasize that Ezekiel is only a human being. God is eternal and powerful, but humans are not. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 2:1](../02/01.md). Alternate translation: “Mortal person” or “Human”

## b22i
Here “arm” represents the powerful army of a king. (See: [[figs-parables]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## dz36
“Pay attention, because what I am about to say is both true and important”

## ts3a
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “No one has bound up his arm” or “No one has wrapped up his arm” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## m4v7
Here “set to heal with a bandage” refers to the arm being wrapped tightly in a bandage so that the parts of the bone will stay together and heal.

## tf9h
These two phrases mean basically the same thing. See how you translated similar phrases in [Ezekiel 12:15](../12/15.md). (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

## bv5e
“among the places in which people live”

# 30:24
## sg7g
“I will make the arms of the king of Babylon strong”

## cq89
“When the king of Babylon comes to attack Egypt, Pharaoh will groan.”

## e6ip
A groan is a noise that people make when they have a lot of pain or are dying.

## y8p3
“as a man who is dying groans” or “like a man who is dying”

# 30:25
## rst2
See [[EZK-rst2]]
## rjv6
Here “arms” are a metonym for the army. Alternate translation: “I will make the army of the king of Babylon strong” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## wjm9
Here “Pharaoh’s arms” is a metonym for his army, and “will fall” represents being weak. Alternate translation: “but Pharaoh’s army will be unable to defeat enemies” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## g2eq
The word “they” possibly refers to (1) “the Egyptians” or (2) “all people who hear what Yahweh has done.”

# 31:intro
## chn5
See [[EZK-chn5]]
# 31:1
## db1f
This phrase is used here to mark the beginning of a new part of the story. If your language has a way for doing this, you could consider using it here.

## t3pb
“in year 11.” This means the eleventh year after King Jehoiachin was taken into exile in Babylon. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 26:1](../26/01.md). Alternate translation: “in the eleventh year of the exile of King Jehoiachin” (See: [[translate-ordinal]] and [[figs-explicit]])

## ghs3
“on the first day of the third month.” This is the third month of the Hebrew calendar. This first day is near the beginning of June on Western calendars. (See: [[translate-hebrewmonths]] and [[translate-ordinal]])

## kcs5
This is an idiom that is used to introduce something that God told his prophets or his people. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 3:16](../03/16.md). Alternate translation: “Yahweh spoke this message” or “Yahweh spoke these words” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 31:2
## b3zm
“Son of a human being” or “Son of humanity.” God calls Ezekiel this to emphasize that Ezekiel is only a human being. God is eternal and powerful, but humans are not. See how you translated this in [Ezekiel 2:1](../02/01.md). Alternate translation: “Mortal person” or “Human”

## d4m7
“Who is as great as you are?” Yahweh uses this question to introduce a new topic. Pharaoh thought that his nation was the greatest, but God is about to talk about another great nation. Alternate translation: “You think that there is no country whose power is as great as the power of your country.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 31:3
## x6wc
See [[EZK-x6wc]]
## ga72
“Look!” or “Listen!” or “Pay attention to what I am about to tell you!”

## a5an
In this parable, God speaks about Assyria as if it were a great tree to emphasize how great and powerful the Assyrians were. This parable continues until 31:9. Alternate translation: “Assyria was like a cedar” (See: [[figs-parables]])

## dm9d
“its top was above the branches of the other trees.” Some versions of the Bible translate this as “the top of the tree was in the clouds.”

# 31:4
## hgu1
“Because the cedar had a lot of water, it grew very tall”

## g9yr
“the water deep in the ground made the cedar grow very big”

## we7t
“and streams flowed from the rivers to all the trees of the field”

# 31:5
## xpc9
See [[EZK-xpc9]]
## i2i5
“The cedar was taller than any of the other trees of the field”

## a33e
“it grew very many branches”

## vt63
“because it had a lot of water”

# 31:6
## p46h
“All kinds of birds that fly in the sky made nests in its branches”

## xq9w
“and all of the creatures that live in the field gave birth under the cedar’s branches”

## v3e6
“All of the great nations lived in the shade of that tree.” Here “nations” represents the people who live there. Alternate translation: “The people from all the nations lived in the shade of that tree” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 31:7
## at82
“It was beautiful because it was very big and its branches were very long”

# 31:8
## v1ux
See [[EZK-v1ux]]
## lcc8
Since these were God’s words to Pharaoh, it can also be translated with the first person. Alternate translation: “The cedar trees in my garden were not as great as that tree” (See: [[figs-123person]])

## n5ax
This is another way of referring to “the garden of Eden.”

## hf16
Yahweh was comparing how many branches the trees had. Alternate translation: “None of the cypress trees had as many branches as the cedar” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## ftl7
Cypress trees have very many branches. They can grow very tall. See how you translated “cypress” in [Ezekiel 27:5](../27/05.md).

## x9pl
It was implied Yahweh was comparing the long branches of the cedar tree with the long branches of the plane tree. Alternate translation: “the plane tree did not have branches like the cedar tree” or “no plane tree had branches as long as the branches of the cedar tree” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## w3nx
A plane tree was a large, leafy tree with strong branches. It is similar to a sycamore tree. (See: [[translate-unknown]])

## cj56
“was as beautiful as the cedar tree”

# 31:9
## gqy2
“I made the cedar beautiful by giving it many long branches”

## bt1v
“were jealous of the cedar tree”

## ki1u

## faz5
